                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/smll.202404039')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="457126188d0946d8aea2f2ccf7955dcb">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '384b3ea5-fedd-4020-9eb9-01d1acc1e74a'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Yan/Haotian">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Chai/Dandan" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a2" data-db-target-for="a2" aria-controls="a2" aria-haspopup="true" id="a2_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Dandan Chai</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a2" aria-labelledby="a2_Ctrl" role="region" id="a2">
                     <p class="author-name">Dandan Chai</p>
                     <p>College of Chemistry, Zhengzhou University, Zhengzhou, 450001 P. R. China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Chai/Dandan">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Li/Xiang" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a3" data-db-target-for="a3" aria-controls="a3" aria-haspopup="true" id="a3_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Xiang Li<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a3" aria-labelledby="a3_Ctrl" role="region" id="a3">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d24505202" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>O3-type cathodes with sufficient Na content are considered as promising candidates for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). However, these cathodes suffer from insufficient utilization of the active elements, restraining the delivered capacity. In this work, a high entropy strategy is applied to a typical O3 cathode NaLi<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.35</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NLNM), forming a high entropy oxide NaLi<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.15</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.35</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (Na-HE). Results show that the active elements are fully exploited in Na-HE, with a two-electron reaction by Ni<sup>2+/4+</sup> (further extended to Cu redox and even oxygen redox), vastly different from a one-electron reaction of Ni<sup>2+/3+</sup> in NLNM. The full utilization of the active elements dramatically improves the output capacity of the cathode (122.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> of Na-HE versus 81 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> of NLNM). Moreover, the detrimental phase transition is well suppressed in Na-HE. The cathode exhibits high capacity retention of 88.7% after 100 cycles at 130&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, compared to only 36.4% for NLNM. These findings provide new insight for the design of new cathode materials for SIBs with high energy density and robust stability.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202404039-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202404039-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion battery (SIB) has become a promising new electrochemical energy storage device, not only because of the advantages of the low cost but also the high safety. Various cathode materials of SIBs have been explored, including oxides,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404039-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0001_R_d24505194e359" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">1</a>-<a href="#smll202404039-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0004_R_d24505194e359" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Sci.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Prussian blue analogues.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404039-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0005_R_d24505194e364" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chem. Commun.">5</a>, <a href="#smll202404039-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0006_R_d24505194e364" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and polyanionic compounds,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404039-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0007_R_d24505194e369" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Mater. Chem. A">7</a>, <a href="#smll202404039-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0008_R_d24505194e369" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc. Among them, layered oxides Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>TMO<sub>2</sub> (TM = transition metals) have been widely studied, especially manganese/iron-based cathode materials. In general, P2 and O3-type cathodes are the two mainly common structures being focused according to the Na<sup>+</sup> concentration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404039-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0009_R_d24505194e382" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">9</a>, <a href="#smll202404039-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0010_R_d24505194e382" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Sci.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Generally speaking, the O3-type cathode with sufficient Na content is considered a promising candidate.</p>
         
         <p>In the O3 structure, the Na<sup>+</sup> is located in the octahedral coordination and diffuses through the tetrahedral position during the charge and discharge process, resulting in the slab gliding. This will produce new phases, inducing tiresome structural evolution and repeated volume change, and further deteriorating the cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404039-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0011_R_d24505194e392" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Mater.">11</a>-<a href="#smll202404039-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0014_R_d24505194e392" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, an effective way has been proposed to achieve good structural stability by regulating TM layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404039-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0015_R_d24505194e397" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Energy Chem.">15</a>-<a href="#smll202404039-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0019_R_d24505194e397" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> as one typical O3-type cathode, suffers from a series of phase transition processes, from O3<sub>hex</sub>–O3<sub>mon</sub>–P3<sub>hex</sub>–P3'<sub>mon</sub>–P3''<sub>hex</sub> during charge and discharge process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404039-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0020_R_d24505194e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Chem. A">20</a>-<a href="#smll202404039-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0022_R_d24505194e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> By doping Ti in TM layers, a reversible and single O3–P3 phase transition process is achieved in NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404039-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0023_R_d24505194e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> By Ti/Cu co-doping to replace Mn/Ni in TM layers of NaNi<sub>0.45</sub>Cu<sub>0.05</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, not only the complex phase transition is effectively suppressed, but also the sensitivity of the material to air is improved.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404039-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0024_R_d24505194e450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sb doping is also applied to stabilize the structure of NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> by adjusting the lattice parameters, avoiding the irreversible phase transition of P3'–O3' during the charge and discharge process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404039-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0025_R_d24505194e462" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Nano">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>However, the other issue of the typical O3-type cathodes is the insufficient utilization of the active elements. For example, Fe/Mn-based NaFe<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> was reported by Yabuuchi et&nbsp;al., delivering ≈150 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (0.6 electron transfer per formula) in the first cycle, which indicates the insufficient utilization of Fe.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404039-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0003_R_d24505194e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ni-based disordered NaNi<sub>2/3</sub>Sb<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> reported by Ma et&nbsp;al., only acquires 0.5 electron transfer per formula, demonstrating the insufficient utilization of Ni (single electron reaction for Ni).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404039-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0026_R_d24505194e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Chem. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fe/Ni-based NaFe<sub>1/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> does not exhibit Fe<sup>4+</sup> at the cut-off voltage of 4&nbsp;V, evidencing the insufficient utilization of Fe.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404039-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0027_R_d24505194e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Mater. Chem. A">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Other active elements such as Co<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404039-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0028_R_d24505194e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> are also found with limited utilization. How to make full use of the active elements is the key to designing a cathode with high energy density.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, a high entropy strategy is employed by regulating the TM component in the O3-type NaLi<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.35</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NLNM) to improve the utilization of the active elements. Therefore, NaLi<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.15</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.35</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (Na-HE) is successfully synthesized by replacing partial Ni<sup>2+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup> in NLMN with Cu<sup>2+</sup>/Mg<sup>2+</sup>, and Ti<sup>4+</sup>, respectively. After multi-component replacement, the configurational entropy value has increased from 1.15 R in NLNM to 1.53 R in Na-HE (R is the universal gas constant).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404039-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0029_R_d24505194e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Eng. Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a result, the exploitation of Ni is fully improved, evidenced by the increased capacity. A two-electron reaction in Na-HE is found, which is very different from the one-electron reaction in NLNM, demonstrating the positive effect of the high entropy strategy. Moreover, the structural stability and rate performances are promoted, contributing a good design for high-energy and stable positive materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202404039-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202404039-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202404039-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202404039-sec-0030-title">2.1 Morphological and Structural Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>Both NLNM and Na-HE were prepared by high-temperature solid-phase method as described in the Methods part. The crystal structures of the synthesized materials were confirmed by X-ray diffraction (XRD). The patterns were refined by the Rietveld method using the General Structural Analysis System (GSAS II) based on the O3-type NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> model.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404039-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0030_R_d24505194e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30">30</a>, <a href="#smll202404039-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0031_R_d24505194e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Appl. Crystallogr.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The computed spectra have a good match with the experimental data. The lattice parameters calculated by NLNM are a = b = 2.93 Å, c = 16.08 Å, α = β = 90° and γ = 120° with a rational low χ<sup>2</sup> (2.92) value. For Na-HE, the lattice parameters are corrected to a = b = 2.96 Å, c = 16.00 Å, α = β = 90° and γ = 120° with goodness-of-fitting parameters of χ<sup>2</sup> (3.11). It was confirmed that both materials crystallized in an O3-type stacking sequence (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202404039-fig-0001">1a,b</a>). The morphology was further characterized by scanning electron microscopy (SEM), which shows a big difference between two materials (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a,b</a>, Supporting Information). Briefly, Na-HE exhibits a large and plate-like morphology while NLNM exhibits small particles. The energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mappings reveal that all the elements are uniformly distributed, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404039-fig-0001">1c</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1c</a> (Supporting Information), proving the successful synthesis.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202404039-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/639f9c35-8b96-473d-8fff-e129ce2342d3/smll202404039-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/639f9c35-8b96-473d-8fff-e129ce2342d3/smll202404039-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5e8b0947-0eab-4ed7-a7b1-6fef73d01969/smll202404039-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/639f9c35-8b96-473d-8fff-e129ce2342d3/smll202404039-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202404039-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202404039" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structure characterization of NLNM and Na-HE. a, b) XRD and Rietveld refinement of a) Na-HE and b) NLNM. c) Energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mappings for elements of Na-HE.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202404039-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202404039-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The Na-HE and NLNM as cathode material were galvanostatically tested with a current density of 13&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> in a potential window of 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). The charge-discharge curves for the first two cycles of both electrodes are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202404039-fig-0002">2a,b</a>. For the NLNM, the specific capacity of the first cycle corresponds to 0.3 e/formula transfer (81 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). Note that the valence of Ni is +2, indicating that the charge specific capacity is less than the capacity based on the redox of Ni<sup>2+/3+</sup> (single electron reaction, 0.35 e/formula transfer, the vertical dotted line in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404039-fig-0002">2a</a>). The charge capacity of Na-HE shows a sharp contrast, corresponding to ≈0.5 e/formula transfer (122.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404039-fig-0002">2b</a>). It should be noticed that there is only 0.15 mole Ni<sup>2+</sup> and 0.1 mole Cu<sup>2+</sup> per formula (active transition metal), which means the charge capacity of Na-HE is larger than the capacity based on the redox of Ni<sup>2+/4+</sup> (2 e reaction) and even the redox of both Ni<sup>2+/4+</sup> and Cu<sup>2+/3+</sup> (total 0.4 e/formula transfer, the vertical dotted line). It is intriguing about the charge compensation mechanism because the high entropy strategy induces the full use of redox species, which will be discussed in the next part. The corresponding dQ/dV and cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves are shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. It is obvious that the NLNM exhibits more redox peaks than Na-HE, demonstrating limited structural evolution by high entropy strategy, which also coincides well with other previous works.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404039-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0023_R_d24505194e674" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#smll202404039-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0032_R_d24505194e674" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202404039-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b6843b4d-a772-4b9b-8e01-330a36e9b023/smll202404039-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b6843b4d-a772-4b9b-8e01-330a36e9b023/smll202404039-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/327266d4-c131-4f96-920a-618e44a5eb19/smll202404039-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b6843b4d-a772-4b9b-8e01-330a36e9b023/smll202404039-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202404039-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202404039" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performances of Na-HE and NLNM as the positive electrodes. Typical initial charge−discharge curves of a) NLNM and b) Na-HE cathodes at 0.1 C (13&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>) during cycling between 2.0 and 4.0&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). The discharge profiles with normalized capacity for the first 40 cycles at the current density of 130&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> of c) NLNM and d) Na-HE. The data were collected every 10 cycles. e) Cycling performance of two electrodes at a high current density of 130&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and the change in the discharge medium voltage over 100 cycles. f) Rate performances of two electrodes when cycled at 0.1, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, and 5.0 C. g) The calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of Na-HE and NLNM for the first cycle during the charging process.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In order to elucidate the difference in stability of the two electrodes, normalized discharged capacities are compared in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404039-fig-0002">2c,d</a>. Obviously, the NLNM shows significant voltage decay for the initial 40 cycles. While Na-HE delivers well overlapped voltage curves. Notably, the voltage fade is clear in a high-voltage region in NLNM, indicating damaged energy densities. Whereas Na-HE maintains stable voltage in the high-voltage region just with attenuated potential in a low voltage region (&lt;≈2.8&nbsp;V), which will be further explained in the next section. Moreover, the long cycling performances of the two materials are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404039-fig-0002">2e</a>, with a clear comparison of average discharge voltage. After 100 cycles at 130&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, Na-HE maintains high capacity retention of 88.7% while the value of NLNM is only 36.4%, showing a vast difference. In the Na-HE system, the average voltage is well maintained at ≈3.0&nbsp;V, with voltage retention of 96.7% after 100 cycles. While for the NLNM system, observed decay appears, with voltage retention of 87.7%. In contrast, the average voltage of NLNM is only 81.7% of Na-HE after 100 cycles (2.40&nbsp;V vs 2.94&nbsp;V), consistent well with the results of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404039-fig-0002">2c,d</a>.</p>
            
            <p>The rates performance of the two electrodes are further compared. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404039-fig-0002">2f</a> shows the discharge capacities at different current densities (0.1, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 C, 1 C = 125&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>). Compared with NLNM, Na-HE exhibits better performance at every rate, indicating a good kinetic behavior obtained by the high entropy strategy. Furthermore, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na+</sub>) was calculated by the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) method, with results shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404039-fig-0002">2g</a> (see details in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The D<sub>Na+</sub> values of Na-HE range from 10<sup>–12</sup> to 10<sup>–9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, which is larger than that of NLNM especially at high voltages. Both results indicate a good Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion behavior induced by a high entropy strategy.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202404039-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202404039-sec-0050-title">2.3 Redox Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>To investigate the charge compensation of Na-HE, ex situ X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurements were first performed for different states of the cathodes. In Mn 2p spectra (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202404039-fig-0003">3a</a>), the binding energy of Mn<sup>4+</sup> in Na-HE is located at 642.5&nbsp;eV, which shows no shift among pristine, 4.0&nbsp;V charged, and 2.85&nbsp;V discharged states, suggesting that Mn clear presents in +4 valence. However, when Na-HE is fully discharged to 2.0&nbsp;V, an obvious peak appears at 647.1&nbsp;eV, marked as a satellite peak, which is a signal of Mn<sup>2+</sup>. The result represents reduced Mn<sup>4+</sup> to Mn<sup>3+</sup> and further to Mn<sup>2+</sup> (disproportionation reaction) at a low voltage range. The Mn<sup>2+</sup> information is further confirmed by inductive coupled plasma (ICP) emission spectroscopic analyzer results (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information), which include large dissolved Mn in the electrolyte. For the NLNM case (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), the binding energy of Mn 2p has no significant peak shift during charge/discharge, indicating that it has been present in the form of Mn<sup>4+</sup>. The difference between the two materials also demonstrates different charge compensation mechanisms. For Ni 2p spectra, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404039-fig-0003">3b</a>, the binding energy of Ni<sup>2+</sup> in Na-HE (initial state) is at 855.1&nbsp;eV, while climbs to 855.9&nbsp;eV after charging to 4.0&nbsp;V which is a signal of Ni<sup>4+</sup>. The Ni<sup>4+</sup> is reduced to Ni<sup>2+</sup> during the discharge process. The difference for Ni redox in NLNM is that the 4.0&nbsp;V charged state shows a Ni<sup>3+</sup> signal rather than Ni<sup>4+</sup>, coinciding well with the specific capacity in charge/discharge curves. The XPS results of other transition metals in Na-HE are compared and shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information), which displays no obvious shift.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202404039-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/913890f1-8d93-43f5-a30a-59eb133ccf60/smll202404039-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/913890f1-8d93-43f5-a30a-59eb133ccf60/smll202404039-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/39241189-62a9-46ba-8746-ea9f07fdbc1d/smll202404039-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/913890f1-8d93-43f5-a30a-59eb133ccf60/smll202404039-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202404039-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202404039" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Redox mechanism of Na-HE. Ex situ XPS analysis of Na-HE. XPS results of a) Mn 2p, b) Ni 2p, c) O 1s spectra Na-HE electrodes. d) Ex situ Raman spectra of Na-HE (powder and 4&nbsp;V charged electrodes). e) Long cycles of Na-HE at different discharge cut-off voltages.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Given that an extra-capacity delivered by Na-HE cathode, O 1s spectra were also collected, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404039-fig-0003">3c</a>. The peak at 530.4&nbsp;eV represents O<sup>2–</sup> (lattice oxygen), shifting to a high-energy region of ≈531&nbsp;eV during charging up to 4.0&nbsp;V, which is a signal of oxygen redox (marked as O<i><sup>n</sup></i><sup>–</sup> in red color) as reported by other works.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404039-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0033_R_d24505194e841" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nat. Mater.">33</a>, <a href="#smll202404039-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0034_R_d24505194e841" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nat. Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The O 1s spectra of NLNM were also given for comparison (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a</a>, Supporting Information), which shows no additional peaks during the charge/discharge process. Moreover, Raman spectroscopy was applied to investigate the information of oxygen dimers due to its powerful ability to detect covalent bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404039-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0035_R_d24505194e850" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Mater.">35</a>, <a href="#smll202404039-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0036_R_d24505194e850" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The spectrum of the pristine and 4.0&nbsp;V charged Na-HE electrode is compared in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404039-fig-0003">3d</a>. It can be seen that there is one small peak appears located at 800–900&nbsp;cm<sup>–1</sup> which corresponds to the O─O stretch region for a 4.0&nbsp;V charged electrode (clear in the inset), consistent with the XPS results and other works.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404039-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0035_R_d24505194e861" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Mater.">35</a>, <a href="#smll202404039-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0037_R_d24505194e861" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ACS Energy Lett.">37</a>, <a href="#smll202404039-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0038_R_d24505194e861" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The results corroborate the different charge compensation mechanisms between the two materials. Briefly, NLNM shows a single-electron reaction through Ni<sup>2+/3+</sup> while Na-HE displays a two-electron reaction through Ni<sup>2+/4+</sup> and further redox of Cu and oxygen. This difference extends to the discharge process, represented by Mn redox appearing in Na-HE while not in NLNM. Considering the effect of Mn redox, the cycling performances of Na-HE with high cutoff voltage (2.7–4.0&nbsp;V, without Mn redox) and low cutoff voltage (2.6–4.0&nbsp;V, with Mn redox) are compared, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404039-fig-0003">3e</a>. There is no obvious decrease in the capacity after 300 cycles for 2.7&nbsp;V discharge, with 94.2% capacity retention. While the capacity decays seriously for 2.6&nbsp;V discharge, with only 58.9% capacity retention. Combined with the XPS results of Mn 2p, the Mn reduction at a low cutoff voltage may be the reason of the performance decay. It should be noted that the Mn reduction usually involves Mn migration and further dissolution, leading to phase transition, as well confirmed by our previous works.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404039-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0039_R_d24505194e873" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Nat. Commun.">39</a>, <a href="#smll202404039-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0040_R_d24505194e873" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202404039-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202404039-sec-0060-title">2.4 Structural Evolution Upon Cycling</h3>
            
            <p>The structural evolution of both materials was then investigated by in situ X-ray diffraction. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202404039-fig-0004">4a</a> shows the first charge-discharge curve of Na-HE accompanied by the XRD color filling pattern (full range pattern in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). During the initial charging process, (003) peak has a shift toward a lower angle, indicating Na<sup>+</sup> extraction. This trend (clear in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404039-fig-0004">4b</a>) continues until charging to 3.6&nbsp;V. Thereafter the material suffers from a phase transition to a P3 phase, represented by the disappearance of the (104) peak. It shows that the Na-HE undergoes a phase transition process from O3–P3 during charging and maintains P3 until discharging to 2.6&nbsp;V, where the P3–O3 process appears.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404039-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404039-bib-0041_R_d24505194e902" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The structural evolution of the second cycle was also shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information), demonstrating good reversibility. In sharp contrast, the NLNM gives a very different structural evolution, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404039-fig-0004">4c,d</a>, with a corresponding full range pattern in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information) (the first and second cycle, respectively). It can be seen that the (104) peak does not disappear during the full charge process, indicating an absent O3–P3 phase transition. However, additional peaks appear during the discharge process at low voltages, indicating mixed phases (O3 and O3'). The co-exist phases reappear in the second cycle, which is believed to be detrimental to the cycling stability.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202404039-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/99c0d694-cdbc-472a-9f1f-83b839269067/smll202404039-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/99c0d694-cdbc-472a-9f1f-83b839269067/smll202404039-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e3776b41-8871-4c58-ab0e-7400b8722591/smll202404039-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/99c0d694-cdbc-472a-9f1f-83b839269067/smll202404039-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202404039-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202404039" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural evolution during the initial charge–discharge process. a, c) 2D contour plots of in situ XRD during the structural evolution of (a) Na-HE and (c) NLNM positive electrodes. b, d) Enlarged image of the main characteristic diffraction peaks of (b) Na-HE and (d) NLNM positive electrodes.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Meanwhile, in order to better understand the structural evolution of Na-HE, the microscopic structures were further observed by scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM) with a high-angle annular dark field (HAADF) model. <b>Figures</b> <a href="#smll202404039-fig-0005">5a–d</a> show the image of the pristine, 4.0&nbsp;V charged, 2.85&nbsp;V discharged, and 2.0&nbsp;V discharged state of the electrodes, respectively, along [010] direction. The bright dots represent TMs because the HAADF model is sensitive to Z-contrast. Figures&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404039-fig-0005">5a–c</a> indicate clear alternant TM and Na slabs, without detected mixing phenomenon. In addition, the lattice spacing is measured to be 5.35 Å for the pristine one and increases to 5.67 Å when charging to 4.0&nbsp;V, which corresponds well to the XRD result. When discharged to 2.85&nbsp;V, the electrode still demonstrates clear slabs without defects. However, the slabs suffer from severe distortion at the 2.0&nbsp;V state, represented by the irregular lattice spacing (coexist of 5.0 and 5.55 Å) and twisty layers (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404039-fig-0005">5d</a>, obvious in the graphs below for the HAADF signal profiles from the rectangle dotted lines). A new peak between two adjacent TM layers emerges as indicated by the black arrows, indicating Mn migration to the sodium layer. This result explains the phenomenon of Mn dissolution in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information). Furthermore, the strain map simulated by geometric phase analysis (GPA) is provided for two discharged electrodes. In contrast, 2.0&nbsp;V discharged one (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404039-fig-0005">5e</a>) exhibits larger stains (red for tensile strain and green for compressive strain) than 2.85&nbsp;V discharged one, indicating a high possibility of structural evolution when discharging to a low voltage. This coincides with the discrepancy in cycling performance in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404039-fig-0003">3e</a>. The structural stability is further investigated. The XRD patterns of the pristine and cycled electrodes are compared (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). It is obvious that the NLNM electrode suffers from a serious phase change while there is no detectable phase change in the Na-HE electrode. Moreover, the materials were soaked in water and compared (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). The results clearly show that the NLNM has obvious stray peaks after three days of soaking, confirming the new phases. While there is no phase transition for Na-HE, indicating that Na-HE has excellent stability against water.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202404039-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9df6854d-a3d1-4457-a2ba-8ba14dfa522d/smll202404039-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9df6854d-a3d1-4457-a2ba-8ba14dfa522d/smll202404039-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1e68b7a1-5b59-4084-b442-2a9d1af6edc8/smll202404039-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9df6854d-a3d1-4457-a2ba-8ba14dfa522d/smll202404039-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202404039-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202404039" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Morphological and microstructural evolution of Na-HE during the initial charge–discharge process. HAADF-STEM images of Na-HE a) Pristine, b) 4&nbsp;V Charged, c) 2.85&nbsp;V discharged, d) 2&nbsp;V discharged. The graphs below are the HAADF signal profiles of the regions enclosed by the dotted lines in the STEM images. e) Corresponding geometric phase analysis (GPA) patterns of c and d. A short summary of f) NLNM and g) Na-HE, which better demonstrates the merits offered by the high entropy strategy. The scale bar: 2&nbsp;nm.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>A short summary is given here about the effect of the high entropy strategy. NLNM shows two annoyed issues (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404039-fig-0005">5f</a>). 1) The first one is limited utilization of TM, with only one-electron reaction of Ni<sup>2+/3+</sup>. 2) The other one is the boring phase transition, degrading the structural stability. While Na-HE benefits from a high entropy strategy with at least four aspects (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404039-fig-0005">5g</a>). 1) The first is the sufficient utilization of TM by a two-electron reaction of Ni<sup>2+/4+</sup> and further extended to Cu redox and 2) even to oxygen redox. 3) The limited phase transition and 4) improved Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion promote the structural and rate stability of the cathode, ensuring a good cycling performance.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202404039-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202404039-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a high entropy strategy is applied in O3 type NaLi<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.15</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.35</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, fully exploiting the element utilization in addition to stabilizing the layered structure. The impact of the high entropy strategy is carefully explored and discovered. First, it extends the elemental utilization. The capacity of Na-HE is obviously improved compared with NLNM, achieving a two-electron transfer reaction which is very different from the typical one-electron transfer reaction in NLNM. Moreover, the oxygen redox is activated by the high entropy strategy which is not detected in NLNM (even though Li-doping in the TM layer). Finally, the kinetic of Na-HE is improved at different rates. As a result, the capacity retention of Na-HE is 88.7% compared to 36.4% for NLNM after 100 cycles at 130&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Meanwhile, the voltage decay is very obvious in NLNM, which is well suppressed in Na-HE. The phase transition in NLNM which is detrimental to the cycling stability is also inhibited after high entropy doping. Our findings provide a new route for designing layered oxide cathodes with high energy density and robust structural stability.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202404039-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202404039-sec-0080-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202404039-sec-0090">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202404039-sec-0100">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202404039-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202404039-sec-0110-title"> Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>All the target compounds were synthesized using a high-temperature solid-state method. Stoichiometric amounts of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (Macklin, 99.5%), Li<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (Aladdin, 99.99%), CuO (Macklin, 99%), NiO (Meryer, 99%), MgO (Aladdin, 99.99%), MnO<sub>2</sub> (Aladdin, 99%), and TiO<sub>2</sub> (Aladdin, 99.8%) were used. All the precursors were stoichiometrically mixed in ethanol (except ≈2% more Li<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and 2% more Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> were added to compensate for possible loss during synthesis) with a Pulverisette 7 small high-speed ball mill at a rate of 220 r.p.m. for 6&nbsp;h. The precursors were then dried in an oven at 60&nbsp;°C overnight and pelletized. Subsequently, all the precursors were sintered at 900&nbsp;°C for 15&nbsp;h in a Muffle furnace. Finally, the materials were cooled to room temperature naturally in the Muffle furnace and transferred to an Ar-filled glovebox.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202404039-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202404039-sec-0120-title"> Electrochemical Tests</h4>
                  
                  <p>2032 coin-type cells were used for electrochemical measurements. NaLi<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.35</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and NaLi<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.15</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.35</sub>O<sub>2</sub> electrode consisted of active material, Super P, and polytetrafluoroethene (PTFE, 12&nbsp;wt.%) binder with the weight ratio of 80:10:10. 1&nbsp;M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC/PC with 5% FEC (ethylene carbonate/propylene carbonate with 5% fluoroethylene carbonate) was prepared as the electrolyte and a glass fiber film was employed as a separator. The galvanostatic charge-discharge tests were performed by using a LANHE 2001A Cell Testing System under different conditions.</p>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">Based on reasonable assumptions, D <sub>Na+</sub> can be determined by introducing Fick's second law of diffusion. Therefore, the equation can be simplified as

                     <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202404039-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                           <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/cf3919b9-ab13-4a00-925d-9a6de7bc7813/smll202404039-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202404039:smll202404039-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/smll202404039-math-0001.png">
                              <semantics>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi mathvariant="normal">D</mi>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi>Na</mi>
                                          <mo>+</mo>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                                    <mfrac>
                                       <mn>4</mn>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi>π</mi>
                                          <mi>τ</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </mfrac>
                                    <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                    <msup>
                                       <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                          <mfrac>
                                             <mrow>
                                                <msub>
                                                   <mi>n</mi>
                                                   <mi>m</mi>
                                                </msub>
                                                <msub>
                                                   <mi>V</mi>
                                                   <mi>m</mi>
                                                </msub>
                                             </mrow>
                                             <mi>S</mi>
                                          </mfrac>
                                       </mfenced>
                                       <mn>2</mn>
                                    </msup>
                                    <msup>
                                       <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                          <mfrac>
                                             <mrow>
                                                <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                                <msub>
                                                   <mi>E</mi>
                                                   <mi>s</mi>
                                                </msub>
                                             </mrow>
                                             <mrow>
                                                <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                                <msub>
                                                   <mi>E</mi>
                                                   <mi>t</mi>
                                                </msub>
                                             </mrow>
                                          </mfrac>
                                       </mfenced>
                                       <mn>2</mn>
                                    </msup>
                                 </mrow>
                                 <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{{{\mathrm{D}}}_{{\mathrm{Na}} + }} = \frac{4}{{\pi \tau }}\ {{\left( {\frac{{{{n}_m}{{V}_m}}}{S}} \right)}^2}{{\left( {\frac{{{{\Delta}}{{E}_s}}}{{{{\Delta}}{{E}_t}}}} \right)}^2}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                              </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div> where τ is the limited time, n<sub>m</sub> is the mole number of the electrode, V<sub>m</sub> is the molar volume of the two materials, S is the area of the electrode, and ΔE<sub>s</sub> and ΔE<sub>t</sub> are the change in steady state potential and the total change during the period of current flux by subtracting the IR drop, respectively. The potential window was 2–4&nbsp;V and was tested at current flow for 1&nbsp;h and then left for 5&nbsp;h to reach a quasi-equilibrium potential.</div>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202404039-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202404039-sec-0130-title"> Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>The morphology of the powder was observed using scanning electron microscopy (SEM, Carl Zeiss Sigma 500 instrument). X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was performed by the Thermo SCIENTIFIC ESCALAB Xi+ with monochromatic Al target (E = 1486.68&nbsp;eV). The vacuum was P &lt; 10<sup>–9</sup> mBar. The binding energy of the samples was corrected for C 1s (284.8&nbsp;eV). For clearly obtain the information, Ar ion etching with an etching depth of 60&nbsp;nm. Raman spectrum was acquired by HORIBA LabRAM HR Evolution was used. Inductively coupled plasma (ICP) data was obtained by ICP-MS: Agilent 7800. The XRD data were obtained by Rigaku SmartLabSE X-ray diffractometer with Cu Kα radiation source. The scanning rate was 2° per min, with a scan range from 10° to 80°. The scanning rate of cells for in situ XRD measurements during cycling was 2° per min, with a scan range from 10° to 60°. High-angle annular dark field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) images were obtained on a double aberration-corrected Thermo scientific Themis Z (3.2) operating at 300&nbsp;kV.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
